<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2016 6:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2016-01-12"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2016 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Orchids make up about 10% of all seed plant species, have great economical value and are of specific scientific interest because of their renowned flowers and ecological adaptations. Here, we report the first draft genome sequence of a lithophytic orchid, Dendrobium catenatum. We predict 28,910 protein-coding genes and find evidence of a whole genome duplication shared with Phalaenopsis. We observed the expansion of many resistance-related genes, suggesting a powerful immune system responsible for adaptation to a wide range of ecological niches. We also discovered extensive duplication of genes involved in glucomannan synthase activities, likely related to the synthesis of medicinal polysaccharides. Expansion of MADS-box gene clades ANR1, StMADS11, and MIKC*, involved in the regulation of development and growth, suggests that these expansions are associated with the astonishing diversity of plant architecture in the genus Dendrobium. On the contrary, members of the type I MADS box gene family are missing, which might explain the loss of the endospermous seed. The findings reported here will be important for future studies into polysaccharide synthesis, adaptations to diverse environments and flower architecture of Orchidaceae."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Orchids make up about 10% of all seed plant species, have great economical value and are of specific scientific interest because of their renowned flowers and ecological adaptations. Here, we report the first draft genome sequence of a lithophytic orchid, <i>Dendrobium catenatum.</i> We predict 28,910 protein-coding genes and find evidence of a whole genome duplication shared with Phalaenopsis. We observed the expansion of many resistance-related genes, suggesting a powerful immune system responsible for adaptation to a wide range of ecological niches. We also discovered extensive duplication of genes involved in glucomannan synthase activities, likely related to the synthesis of medicinal polysaccharides. Expansion of MADS-box gene clades <i>ANR1</i>, <i>StMADS11,</i> and <i>MIKC</i><sup>*</sup>, involved in the regulation of development and growth, suggests that these expansions are associated with the astonishing diversity of plant architecture in the genus <i>Dendrobium</i>. On the contrary, members of the type I MADS box gene family are missing, which might explain the loss of the endospermous seed. The findings reported here will be important for future studies into polysaccharide synthesis, adaptations to diverse environments and flower architecture of Orchidaceae.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Orchids, constituting approximately 10% of all seed plant species, have enormous value for commercial horticulture and are of specific scientific interest because of their spectacular flowers, ecological adaptations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Roberst, D. L. D. &amp; Orchids, K. W. Curr Biol 18, 325–329 (2008)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1134">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Pridgeon A. M., Cribb, J. P., Chase W. M. &amp; Rasmussen F. N. In Genera Orchidacearum, Vol. 6, Epidendroideae (Part Three), 3–544 (Oxford University Press, Oxford, UK, 2013)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1137">2</a></sup> and secondary metabolites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Leitch, I. J. et al. Genome size diversity in orchids: consequences and evolution. Ann Bot 104, 469–481 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1141">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Ng, T. B. et al. Review of research on Dendrobium, a prized folk medicine. Appl Microbiol Biot 93, 1795–1803 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1144">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Pan, L. H. et al. Comparison of hypoglycemic and antioxidative effects of polysaccharides from four different Dendrobium species. Int J Biol Macromol 64, 420–427 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1147">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Dressler, R. L. In Phylogeny and Classification of the Orchid Family. 7–278 (Cambridge University Press, Australia, 1993)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1150">6</a></sup>. <i>Dendrobium</i> is the third largest genus of Orchidaceae and contains approximately 1,450 species, characterised by a fleshy stem with abundant polysaccharides and growing in diverse habitats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Leitch, I. J. et al. Genome size diversity in orchids: consequences and evolution. Ann Bot 104, 469–481 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1157">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Ng, T. B. et al. Review of research on Dendrobium, a prized folk medicine. Appl Microbiol Biot 93, 1795–1803 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1160">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Pan, L. H. et al. Comparison of hypoglycemic and antioxidative effects of polysaccharides from four different Dendrobium species. Int J Biol Macromol 64, 420–427 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1163">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Dressler, R. L. In Phylogeny and Classification of the Orchid Family. 7–278 (Cambridge University Press, Australia, 1993)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1166">6</a></sup>. A draft genome sequence of <i>Dendrobium officinale</i> Kimura &amp; Migo has been reported before but the highly fragmented assembly and the presence of multiple peaks in <i>K</i>-mer analyses, suggesting that its sequence is likely derived from an artificial hybrid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yan, L. et al. The genome of Dendrobium officinale illuminates biology of the important traditional Chinese orchid herb. Mol Plant 10.1016/j.molp.2014.12.011 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1177">7</a></sup>, seriously complicate correct interpretation of the genome. To complement the lack of a high quality, well assembled genome sequence for <i>Dendrobium</i>, we here present the genome of <i>D. catenatum</i> Lindl., a lithophytic orchid found in subtropical and temperate regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Pridgeon A. M., Cribb, J. P., Chase W. M. &amp; Rasmussen F. N. In Genera Orchidacearum, Vol. 6, Epidendroideae (Part Three), 3–544 (Oxford University Press, Oxford, UK, 2013)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1187">2</a></sup> and commonly used as a health food in many Asian countries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Leitch, I. J. et al. Genome size diversity in orchids: consequences and evolution. Ann Bot 104, 469–481 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1191">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Ng, T. B. et al. Review of research on Dendrobium, a prized folk medicine. Appl Microbiol Biot 93, 1795–1803 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1194">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Pan, L. H. et al. Comparison of hypoglycemic and antioxidative effects of polysaccharides from four different Dendrobium species. Int J Biol Macromol 64, 420–427 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1197">5</a></sup>. Analysis of the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome sequence offers insights into flower development and polysaccharide synthesis, as well as its wide distribution.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and genome characteristics</h3><p><i>Dendrobium catenatum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1</a>) has thirty-eight (2N = 2X = 38) small chromosomes of approximately 2 μm (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). To sequence its complete genome, we generated a total of 222.51 Gb of raw reads, with multiple insert libraries ranging in size from 180 bp to 20 Kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 1</a>). A <i>K</i>-mer analysis estimated the genome size of <i>D. catenatum</i> at 1.11 Gb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). Assembly was done with SOAPdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. GigaScience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1241">8</a></sup> and Platanus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res 24, 1384–1395 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1245">9</a></sup>, but completeness and N50 length of scaffolds were much better with the latter tool (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>), the results of which were used in subsequent analyses. The total length of its assembly was 1.01 Gb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>). Mapping all of the paired-end reads to the assembly revealed that 97% of the sequence had a coverage depth greater than five (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). Further quality analysis indicated that 93% of the set of eukaryotic core genes (CEGMA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1259">10</a></sup> were present and 97% were partially represented, suggesting near completeness of the euchromatin component. In addition, 93%–95% of the RNA seq data set could be mapped onto the assembled sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">4</a>). These results suggest that our genome assembly is of high quality.</p><p>A total of 789 Mb of repetitive elements occupying more than 78.1% of the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome were annotated using a method combining structural and homology information. Retrotransposable elements, known to be the dominant form of repeats in angiosperm genomes, constituted a large part of the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome and included the most abundant subtypes, such as LTR/Copia (27.36%), LTR/Gypsy (18.49%), LINE/L1 (8.44%) and LINE/RTE (5.68%), among others. In addition, the percentage of <i>de novo</i> predicted repeats was notably larger than that obtained for repeats based on Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1281">11</a></sup>, indicating that <i>D. catenatum</i> has many unique repeats compared to other sequenced plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Among these elements, long terminal repeats (LTRs) were the most dominant type, accounting for approximately 46% of the genome. After calculating their times of insertion, we discovered that a burst of LTR activity occurred during the last five million years (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>) and therefore, we deduced that these LTRs were inserted into the genome after <i>D. catenatum</i> diverged from <i>Phalaenopsis</i> species (which is estimated to have occurred 22.6–59.6 million years ago, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). We annotated 28,910 protein-coding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 4</a>), of which 22,394 (74.9%) were supported by transcriptome data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 6</a>). Notably, we found that <i>D. catenatum</i> has, on average, longer genes than most other sequenced plant species, although similar to that of the butterfly orchid <i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i> (Shauer) Rchb. f.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat Genet 47, 65–72 (2015)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1323">12</a></sup>, due to both species having longer average intron lengths (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 7</a>). Therefore, this feature might be a unique characteristic of Orchidaceae. In addition, we identified 49 microRNAs, 310 transfer RNAs, 248 ribosomal RNAs and 144 small nuclear RNAs in the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19029/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19029/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19029_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19029/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19029_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1115" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Phylogenetic position and Ks distributions for <i>D. catenatum</i>.</p><p>(<b>a</b>) Phylogenetic tree showing the topology and divergence times for 12 plant species, including <i>D. catenatum</i>. Estimated divergence times are indicated by light blue boxes at internodes. Numbers at nodes indicate bootstrap values. The brown bar indicates the orchid-specific whole-genome duplication (WGD), while the green bar indicates a more ancient monocot-specific WGD (Thanks Li-Jun Chen for taking the images of species). (<b>b</b>) Distribution of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (Ks) for the whole <i>D. catenatum</i> paranome. (<b>c</b>) Distribution of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (Ks) for orthologous genes found in syntenic regions. Two consistent peaks highlighted by the dashed rectangles are considered to reflect the most recent and older WGD events.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19029/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We determined the expansion and contraction of orthologous protein families among species using CAFÉ2.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J. P. &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269–1271 (2006)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1382">13</a></sup>, which is based on a probabilistic graphical model. For each species, expanded and contracted (compared with their ancestors) gene families were compared with <i>D. catenatum</i> to identify gene families that were uniquely expanded or contracted in <i>D. catenatum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 5</a>). Seven hundred and fifty-six gene families were found to be expanded in <i>D. catenatum</i> (30 of these significantly) and 804 families contracted (of which four significantly; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). For the significantly expanded gene families, we conducted GO enrichment analysis and found enrichment for the GO terms ‘DNA metabolic process’, ‘cellular macromolecule metabolic process’, ‘RNA-directed DNA polymerase activity’, ‘primary metabolic process’ and ‘ribonuclease H activity’ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 9</a>).</p><p>We identified 5,758,781 heterozygous single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) in the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome. The heterozygous SNP rate for the whole genome was estimated at 6.28 × 10<sup>–3</sup>, whereas the SNP rate in exons was as low as 4.98 × 10<sup>–3</sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). Of the 139,830 SNPs that were found in exons, 69,459 caused non-synonymous mutations, affecting 18,404 genes and this suggested that <i>D. catenatum</i> is a high heterozygosity genome. We conducted a Gene Ontology (GO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. The Gene Ontology Consortium. Nat Genet 25, 25–29 (2000)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1422">14</a></sup> and KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ogata, H. et al. KEGG: Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes. Nucleic Acids Res 27, 29–34 (1999)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1426">15</a></sup> enrichment analyses of the affected genes and found enrichment of the KEGG pathways ‘Biosynthesis of secondary metabolites’, ‘Plant hormone signal transduction’, ‘Metabolic pathways’ and ‘Isoflavonoid biosynthesis’(<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 10</a>) and the GO terms ‘ATP binding’, ‘protein tyrosine kinase activity’ and ‘transition metal ion binding’ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 11</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome evolution</h3><p>We constructed a highly supported phylogenetic tree and estimated the divergence times of 12 plants based on genes extracted from a total of 677 single-copy families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). As expected, we found that the <i>D. catenatum</i> is most closely related to <i>P. equestris</i> from which it separated approximately 38 million years ago.</p><p>Both the distribution of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (Ks) across all paralogous genes (regardless of gene order, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>) and for duplicated genes lying in synteny blocks (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>) show two obvious peaks at Ks values between 0.7–0.9 and 1.5–1.8, suggestive of two rounds of whole-genome duplication (WGDs) in the <i>D. catenatum</i> lineage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 6</a>). Dating of the WGDs suggests that the most recent WGD appeared near to the Cretaceous–Paleogene (K/Pg) boundary<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Fawcett, J. A., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Plants with double genomes might have had a better chance to survive the Cretaceous-Tertiary extinction event. Proc Natl Acad Sci USA 106, 5737–5742 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1468">16</a></sup> and is shared with the WGD event documented recently for <i>P. equestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat Genet 47, 65–72 (2015)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1475">12</a></sup>. Since it has been suggested that WGDs might facilitate species diversification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Van de Peer, Y., Maere, S. &amp; Meyer, A. The evolutionary significance of ancient genome duplications. Nat Rev Genet 10, 725–732 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1479">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Oderda, G. M. et al. Creating a path to the summit by thinking off the map: report of the 2008-2009 Academic Affairs Committee. Am J Pharm Educ 73 Suppl, S7 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1482">18</a></sup>, it would be interesting to see whether the WGD has also been shared with the species-rich subfamily Orchidoideae (3630 species), which diverged from the Epidendroideae (about 20,000 species, amongst which <i>D. catenatum</i> and <i>P. equestris</i>) about 59 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Gustafsson, A. L., Verola, C. F. &amp; Antonelli, A. Reassessing the temporal evolution of orchids with new fossils and a Bayesian relaxed clock, with implications for the diversification of the rare South American genus Hoffmannseggella (Orchidaceae: Epidendroideae). BMC Evol Biol 10, 177 (2010)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1492">19</a></sup> and with the subfamilies Apostasioideae, Vanilloideae and Cypripedioideae, which only include 17, 185 and 180 species, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="The Angiosperm Phylogeny, G. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG III. Bot J Linn Soc 161, 105–121 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1497">20</a></sup>. Cypripedioideae and the ancestor of Orchidoideae and Epidendroideae subfamilies are assumed to have diverged from each other about 68 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Gustafsson, A. L., Verola, C. F. &amp; Antonelli, A. Reassessing the temporal evolution of orchids with new fossils and a Bayesian relaxed clock, with implications for the diversification of the rare South American genus Hoffmannseggella (Orchidaceae: Epidendroideae). BMC Evol Biol 10, 177 (2010)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1501">19</a></sup>. Unfortunately, whole genome sequences, or extensive transcriptome data sets from members of these other subfamilies are not yet available. The older peak in the Ks age distribution probably points to one or more older WGD events that have occurred in the monocot lineage, as already previously suggested<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Salse, J. In silico archeogenomics unveils modern plant genome organisation, regulation and evolution. Curr Opin Plant Biol 15, 122–130 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1505">21</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene family evolution</h3><p>We have also zoomed in on some specific gene families.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec6">Terpene synthase genes</h4><p>As secondary metabolites, most plant terpenes and their corresponding synthases have evolved selectively to increase fitness by adaptation to specific ecological niches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Chen, F., Tholl, D., Bohlmann, J. &amp; Pichersky, E. The family of terpene synthases in plants: a mid-size family of genes for specialized metabolism that is highly diversified throughout the kingdom. Plant J 66, 212–229 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1523">22</a></sup>. Plant terpene synthase (<i>TPS</i>) genes can be divided into seven subfamilies (<i>a, b, c, d, e/f, g</i> and <i>h</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Chen, F., Tholl, D., Bohlmann, J. &amp; Pichersky, E. The family of terpene synthases in plants: a mid-size family of genes for specialized metabolism that is highly diversified throughout the kingdom. Plant J 66, 212–229 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1536">22</a></sup>. The <i>TPS</i> genes of <i>D. catenatum</i> and <i>P. equestris</i> all fall into known angiosperm <i>TPS</i> clades, <i>TPS-a</i>, <i>TPS-b</i>, <i>TPS-e/f</i>, <i>TPS-c</i> and <i>TPS-g</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). The genome of <i>D. catenatum</i> encodes 39 members of <i>TPS</i>, whereas there are only 21 in the genome of <i>P. equestris.</i> Notably, rapid expansion by tandem gene duplication is particularly common in the <i>TPS</i>-a subfamily of these two orchids. Furthermore, the specific placement of <i>TPS-a</i> genes for <i>D. catenatum</i> and <i>P. equestris</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM2">Supplementary Tables 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM3">13</a>) implies that the expansion of this gene family has occurred in the ancestor of the Epidendroideae subfamily, or at least prior to the divergence of <i>D. catenatum</i> and <i>P. equestris</i> and might have contributed to species radiation in this subfamily, containing over 20,000 species. Indeed, although this needs to be further investigated, a previous study has suggested that the expansion of the <i>TPS-a</i> subfamily might be linked to the radiation of the flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science 342, 1241089 (2013)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1613">23</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19029/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19029/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19029_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19029/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19029_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="481" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Phylogeny of putative full-length TPSs from <i>D. catenatum</i> (green), <i>P. equestris</i> (purple), <i>O. sativa</i> (blue) and <i>A. thaliana</i> (cyan).</p><p>See text for details.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19029/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec7">Disease resistance genes</h4><p>Plant disease resistance genes (<i>R</i> genes) play a key role in recognizing proteins expressed by specific avirulence genes of pathogens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Salse, J. In silico archeogenomics unveils modern plant genome organisation, regulation and evolution. Curr Opin Plant Biol 15, 122–130 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1661">21</a></sup> and form various subfamilies, such as the TIR-domain-containing (for example, TOLL/INTERLEUKIN LIKE RECEPTOR/RESISTANCE PROTEIN) (TIR-NB-LRR), the non-TIR-domain containing (NB-LRR) and the non-TIR coiled-coil domain-containing (CC-NB-LRR) R-protein subfamilies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="McHale, L., Tan, X., Koehl, P. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. Plant NBS-LRR proteins: adaptable guards. Genome Biol 7, 212 (2006)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1665">24</a></sup>. The genomes of <i>D. catenatum</i> and <i>P. equestris</i> possess 157 and 79 <i>R</i> genes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 14</a>). Although further investigation is required, the dramatic expansion of its <i>R</i> genes suggests that <i>D. catenatum</i> may possess a more powerful disease immune system than <i>P. equestris</i>.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec8">Heat-shock proteins</h4><p>As molecular chaperones, heat-shock proteins (Hsp) are ubiquitous in plant cells. Hsp genes are not only associated with stress caused by heat shock and other abiotic factors, but have recently also been found to be associated with response to biotic stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wang, W., Vinocur, B., Shoseyov, O. &amp; Altman, A. Role of plant heat-shock proteins and molecular chaperones in the abiotic stress response. Trends Plant Sci 9(5), 244–252 (2004)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1699">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Rensing, S. A. et al. The Physcomitrella genome reveals evolutionary insights into the conquest of land by plants. Science 319, 64–69 (2008)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1702">26</a></sup>. Hsp genes function to manage the stress-induced denaturation of other proteins and can be classified into seven major families based on their molecular weight: small Hsps, Hsp20, Hsp40, Hsp60, Hsp70, Hsp90 and Hsp110. Of those, plants mainly contain Hsp20, Hsp70 and Hsp90 subfamilies. The genome of <i>D. catenatum</i> contains 20 members of Hsp70, whereas there are only 9 in that of <i>P. equestris.</i> Interestingly, in particular Hsp70 genes encoding proteins localizing in the cytoplasm have more members in <i>D. catenatum</i> than in <i>P. equestris</i> (11 vs. 3) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Due to the fact that <i>D. catenatum</i> is found in subtropical and temperate regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>), can grow in wet and dry environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Ng, T. B. et al. Review of research on Dendrobium, a prized folk medicine. Appl Microbiol Biot 93, 1795–1803 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1728">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Duan, J. &amp; Duan, Y. P. In Cultivative Technology of Dendrobium catenatum. 1–151 (Fujian Science and Technology Press, Fuzhou, China, 2013)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1731">27</a></sup>, can tolerate both low and high temperatures<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Ng, T. B. et al. Review of research on Dendrobium, a prized folk medicine. Appl Microbiol Biot 93, 1795–1803 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1735">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Duan, J. &amp; Duan, Y. P. In Cultivative Technology of Dendrobium catenatum. 1–151 (Fujian Science and Technology Press, Fuzhou, China, 2013)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1738">27</a></sup> and has a much wider distribution than <i>P. equestris</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>), it is interesting to speculate that the additional Hsp70 genes in the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome might have helped in the adaptation to a much wider variety of environments. However, more future work will be necessary to prove or disprove this hypothesis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Evolution of polysaccharide synthase gene families</h3><p>The fleshy stem of <i>D. catenatum</i> contains various types of polysaccharides, many of which have medicinal, such as anti-inflammatory, immuno-enhancing, antioxidant and anti-glycation activities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Ng, T. B. et al. Review of research on Dendrobium, a prized folk medicine. Appl Microbiol Biot 93, 1795–1803 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1764">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Pan, L. H. et al. Comparison of hypoglycemic and antioxidative effects of polysaccharides from four different Dendrobium species. Int J Biol Macromol 64, 420–427 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1767">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wang, J. H., Zha, X. Q., Luo, J. P. &amp; Yang, X. F. An acetylated galactomannoglucan from the stems of Dendrobium nobile Lindl. Carbohyd Res 345, 1023–1027 (2010)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1770">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Hsieh, Y. S. et al. Structure and bioactivity of the polysaccharides in medicinal plant Dendrobium huoshanense. Bioorg Med Chem 16(11), 6054–6068 (2008)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1773">29</a></sup>. Among those, particularly glucomannan (GM) and galactoglucomannan (GGM) are two major medicinal polysaccharides in <i>D. catenatum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Xing, X. et al. A review of isolation process, structural characteristics and bioactivities of water-soluble polysaccharides from Dendrobium plants. Bioact Carbohydrates Dietary Fibre 1, 131–147 (2013)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1779">30</a></sup>. Genes involved in GM and GGM biosynthesis were identified through their homology with genes in the Arabidopsis genome. A biosynthetic pathway was proposed and the tissue-specific expression patterns of GM and GGM biosynthesis genes were examined<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Diao, Y. et al. De novo transcriptome and small RNA analyses of two amorphophallus species. PLoS ONE 9, e95428 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1783">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Gille, S. et al. Deep sequencing of voodoo lily (Amorphophallus konjac): an approach to identify relevant genes involved in the synthesis of the hemicellulose glucomannan. Planta 234, 515–526 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1786">32</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#Fig3">Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 7</a>). The result suggests that the downstream genes of the biosynthesis pathway are highly expressed in stem tissues where high levels of GM and GGM accumulate. Therefore, we focussed on the analysis of these genes in the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19029/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19029/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19029_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep19029/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep19029_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="930" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Proposed biosynthetic pathway of GM and GGM in <i>Dendrobium</i> stem.</p><p>The biosynthetic pathway was modified according to the pathways proposed in <i>Amorphophallus konjac</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Diao, Y. et al. De novo transcriptome and small RNA analyses of two amorphophallus species. PLoS ONE 9, e95428 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1818">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Gille, S. et al. Deep sequencing of voodoo lily (Amorphophallus konjac): an approach to identify relevant genes involved in the synthesis of the hemicellulose glucomannan. Planta 234, 515–526 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1821">32</a></sup>. GM or GGM biosynthesis is supposed to be generated from sucrose, mainly produced by photosynthesis in the leaf tissue (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 7</a>). The enzymes indicated in red are highly expressed in the stem. Only abbreviations of gene names are shown: <i>Csl</i>, Cellulose synthase like gene; FRK, fructokinase; Fru, fructose; Fru-6-P, Fructose-6-phosphate; GGP, GDP-glucose-pyrophosphorylase; GMPP, GDP-mannose pyrophosphorylase; GT, glycosyltransferase; HXK, hexokinase; INV, invertase; MSR, mannan synthesis-related; PGI, phosphoglucose isomerase; PGM, phosphoglucomutase; PMI, phosphomannose isomerase; PMM, phosphomannomutase; RWA, Reduced Wall Acetylation proten; SuS, sucrose synthase; UGE, UDP-galactose epimerase; UGP, UDP-glucose pyrophosphorylase.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep19029/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Since GM or GGM polysaccharides of <i>D. catenatum</i> are easily extracted with water, they may not be tightly bound to the cell wall and probably act as storage polysaccharides in specialized mucilage cells rather than being structural polysaccharides<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wang, J. H., Zha, X. Q., Luo, J. P. &amp; Yang, X. F. An acetylated galactomannoglucan from the stems of Dendrobium nobile Lindl. Carbohyd Res 345, 1023–1027 (2010)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1845">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Xing, X. et al. A review of isolation process, structural characteristics and bioactivities of water-soluble polysaccharides from Dendrobium plants. Bioact Carbohydrates Dietary Fibre 1, 131–147 (2013)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1848">30</a></sup>. Konjac glucomannan (KGM) is water-soluble and accumulates in storage tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Chua, M., Hocking, T. J., Chan, K. &amp; Baldwin, T. C. Temporal and spatial regulation of glucomannan deposition and mobilization in corms of Amorphophallus konjac (Araceae). Am J Bot 100, 337–345 (2013)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1852">33</a></sup>. It also has several bioactivities, such as reducing plasma cholesterol, removing free radicals and inhibiting tumor genesis and metastasis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Yao-ling, L., Rong-hua, D., Ni, C., Juan, P. &amp; Jie, P. Review of Konjac Glucomannan: Isolation, Structure, Chain Conformation and Bioactivities. J Single Mol Res 1, 7–14 (2013)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1856">34</a></sup>. In addition, the backbone structure of KGM is similar to that of GM. Therefore, we included konjac EST sequences responsible for GM synthesis to search for <i>D. catenatum</i> orthologs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Gille, S. et al. Deep sequencing of voodoo lily (Amorphophallus konjac): an approach to identify relevant genes involved in the synthesis of the hemicellulose glucomannan. Planta 234, 515–526 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1864">32</a></sup>.</p><p>Previous studies showed that <i>CslA</i> (<i>Cellulose synthase-like A</i>) genes of glycosyltransferase (GT) family 2 are involved in GM backbone synthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Dhugga, K. S. et al. Guar seed beta-mannan synthase is a member of the cellulose synthase super gene family. Science 303, 363–366 (2004)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1877">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Verhertbruggen, Y., Yin, L., Oikawa, A. &amp; Scheller, H. V. Mannan synthase activity in the CSLD family. Plant Signal Behav 6, 1620–1623 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1880">36</a></sup>. We found 13 <i>CslA</i> genes in the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome, compared to only 6 copies in the <i>P. equestris</i> genome. This expansion of <i>CslA</i> genes in the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome is mainly due to tandem duplication (three arrays: <i>Dca006365</i>, <i>Dca006366</i>; <i>Dca007032</i>, <i>Dca007033</i>, <i>Dca007034</i>; <i>Dca013434</i>, <i>Dca013437</i>). Interestingly, these genes were grouped in the same clade with <i>AkCslA3</i>, a konjac GM synthase (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). In addition, two of these genes (<i>Dca006366</i> and <i>Dca007032</i>) were significantly higher expressed in stem than in other tissues (root, leaf and flower, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). Therefore, these expanded <i>CslA</i> genes may act as GM or GGM synthases in <i>D. catenatum</i>. Although <i>CslD</i> genes were reported to synthesize mannan rather than GM in <i>A. thaliana</i>, a recent study showed that konjac <i>CslD</i> may also be involved in the synthesis of GM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Diao, Y. et al. De novo transcriptome and small RNA analyses of two amorphophallus species. PLoS ONE 9, e95428 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1954">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Verhertbruggen, Y., Yin, L., Oikawa, A. &amp; Scheller, H. V. Mannan synthase activity in the CSLD family. Plant Signal Behav 6, 1620–1623 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1957">36</a></sup>. Based on our phylogenetic analysis, two <i>D. catenatum</i> genes (<i>Dca018361</i> and <i>Dca000653</i>) cluster with the konjac <i>CslD</i> EST clones (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). These two genes were highly expressed in stem and leaf, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>) and suggest their potential roles in the synthesis of GM or GGM.</p><p>Arabidopsis <i>CslD5</i> was reported to play an important role in osmotic stress tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Zhu, J. et al. A cellulose synthase-like protein is required for osmotic stress tolerance in Arabidopsis. Plant J 63(1), 128–140 (2010)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1986">37</a></sup>. In addition, GM present in the pseudobulb of an epiphytic CAM orchid, <i>Cattleya forbesii</i> Lindl. × <i>Laelia tenebrosa</i> Rolfe, has been associated with drought tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Stancatoa, G. C., Mazzafera, P. &amp; Buckeridge, M. S. Effect of a drought period on the mobilization of non-structural carbohydrates, photosynthetic efficiency and water status in an epiphytic orchid. Plant Physiol Bioch 39, 1009–1016 (2001)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d54135e1996">38</a></sup>. The large accumulation of starch, fructan and GM in storage organs of geophytes is critical for their survival in detrimental conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ranwala, A. P. &amp; Miller, W. B. Analysis of nonstructural carbohydrates in storage organs of 30 ornamental geophytes by high-performance anion-exchange chromatography with pulsed amperometric detection. New Phytol 180(2), 421–433 (2008)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2001">39</a></sup>. Because <i>Dendrobium</i> species accumulate high amounts of GM and GGM in their stems and/or leaves, it would be interesting to know whether this is also related to adaptation to environmental stresses, such as drought, a common condition experienced by epiphytic or lithophytic <i>Dendrobium</i> species in their natural environment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Fan, H. H. et al. Effects of exogenous nitric oxide on antioxidation and DNA methylation of Dendrobium huoshanense grown under drought stress. Plant Cell Tiss Org 109, 307–314 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2011">40</a></sup>. The online microarray data from Arabidopsis eFP Browser seems to support this because <i>A. thaliana CslA</i> (<i>CslA7</i> and <i>CslA10</i>) and <i>CslD</i> (<i>CslD2</i> and <i>CslD3</i>) genes are induced by drought, osmotic, salt or cold stress (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>).</p><p>Storage of carbohydrates in geophytes can serve as carbon and energy sources for the maintenance under adverse environments and for growth under favorable conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ranwala, A. P. &amp; Miller, W. B. Analysis of nonstructural carbohydrates in storage organs of 30 ornamental geophytes by high-performance anion-exchange chromatography with pulsed amperometric detection. New Phytol 180(2), 421–433 (2008)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2041">39</a></sup>. In addition, soluble sugars, such as glucose and sucrose, can act as osmolytes under osmotic stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Rosa, M. et al. Soluble sugars–metabolism, sensing and abiotic stress: a complex network in the life of plants. Plant Signal Behav 4(5), 388–393 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2045">41</a></sup>. Accumulation of these metabolites is enhanced in response to environmental stresses and has been shown to contribute to drought and freezing tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Rosa, M. et al. Soluble sugars–metabolism, sensing and abiotic stress: a complex network in the life of plants. Plant Signal Behav 4(5), 388–393 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2049">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Mattana, M. et al. Overexpression of Osmyb4 enhances compatible solute accumulation and increases stress tolerance of Arabidopsis thaliana. Physiol Plantarum 125, 212–223 (2005)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2052">42</a></sup>. In Easter lily bulbs, when stored at –1.0 °C, large amounts of sucrose, mannose, fructose and oligosaccharides accumulated, suggesting that not only starch but also GM was degraded to soluble sugars during frozen storage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Miller, W. B. &amp; Langhans, R. W. Low temperature alters carbohydrate metabolism in Easter lily bulbs. HortScience 25(4), 463–465 (1990)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2056">43</a></sup>. Therefore, degradation of GM and GGM to monomers in <i>Dendrobium</i> stems might also be induced by stress and play a role in increasing tolerance for drought, cold, salt and osmosis. GM or GGM were reported to be hydrolysed by glycosyl hydrolase families 5 (GH5) enzymes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Hrmova, M. et al. Hydrolysis of (1,4)-β-D-mannans in barley (Hordeum vulgare L.) is mediated by the concerted action of (1,4)- β-D-mannan endohydrolase and β-D-mannosidase. Biochem J 399(1), 77–90 (2006)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2064">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Schroder, R., Wegrzyn, T. F., Sharma, N. N. &amp; Atkinson, R. G. LeMAN4 endo-beta-mannanase from ripe tomato fruit can act as a mannan transglycosylase or hydrolase. Planta 224(5), 1091–1102 (2006)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2067">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Wang, Y., Vilaplana, F., Brumer, H. &amp; Aspeborg, H. Enzymatic characterization of a glycoside hydrolase family 5 subfamily 7 (GH5_7) mannanase from Arabidopsis thaliana. Planta 239(3), 653–665 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2070">46</a></sup>. We thus performed a phylogenetic analysis of <i>GH5</i> genes and analysed their tissue-specific gene expression. As the data show, several <i>DcaGH5</i> genes were expressed at higher levels in the stems (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>). Among these genes, <i>Dca014977</i> clusters with <i>LeMAN4</i>, <i>HvMAN1</i> and <i>AtMAN1</i>, which have been demonstrated to possess hydrolytic activities to GM and GGM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Hrmova, M. et al. Hydrolysis of (1,4)-β-D-mannans in barley (Hordeum vulgare L.) is mediated by the concerted action of (1,4)- β-D-mannan endohydrolase and β-D-mannosidase. Biochem J 399(1), 77–90 (2006)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2096">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Schroder, R., Wegrzyn, T. F., Sharma, N. N. &amp; Atkinson, R. G. LeMAN4 endo-beta-mannanase from ripe tomato fruit can act as a mannan transglycosylase or hydrolase. Planta 224(5), 1091–1102 (2006)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2099">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Wang, Y., Vilaplana, F., Brumer, H. &amp; Aspeborg, H. Enzymatic characterization of a glycoside hydrolase family 5 subfamily 7 (GH5_7) mannanase from Arabidopsis thaliana. Planta 239(3), 653–665 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2102">46</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>). Interestingly, the expression of <i>AtMAN1</i> and <i>OsMAN4</i>, the rice GH5 gene that grouped with <i>HvMAN1</i>, was significantly induced by cold, osmotic, salt or drought stress (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>). All together, these results strongly suggest that the biological functions of GM or GGM in storage organs of <i>D. catenatum</i> are related to environmental stress tolerance.</p><p>A genome-wide analysis of 12 previously sequenced plant genomes and subsequent KEGG enrichment analysis of the 629 <i>D. catenatum</i> specific gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Figs 20</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">21</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 15</a>) showed that the functional pathways of these unique families were significantly enriched in ‘Tyrosine metabolism’, ‘Fatty acid metabolism’ and ‘Glycolysis/Gluconeogenesis’ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 16</a>). Intriguingly, the <i>D. catenatum</i> specific genes implicated in the ‘Glycolysis/Gluconeogenesis’ pathway could help to shape and maintain the physiological mechanism that synthesises and stores polysaccharides in the stem.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Evolution of MADS-box genes</h3><p>Given that orchids are a unique model system for flower development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat Genet 47, 65–72 (2015)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2156">12</a></sup>, we characterised their MADS-box genes, which hold diverse functions in many important processes during plant development, in greater detail. An investigation of the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome revealed 63 putative functional MADS-box genes and 12 pseudogenes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#Tab1">Table 1</a>). As earlier reported for <i>P. equestris</i>, there seem to be fewer MADS box genes present in orchids than in most other angiosperms, such as rice (<i>Oryza sativa;</i> 75 genes) and <i>A. thaliana</i> (108 genes). <i>D. catenatum</i> has 35 type II MADS-box genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#Tab1">Table 1</a>), compared with 29 in <i>P. equestris</i>. Phylogenetic analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 22</a>) shows that most type II MADS-box genes have been duplicated in <i>D. catenatum</i>, except for those in the B-<i>PI</i> clade. Among these clades, <i>ANR1</i> (with three members), <i>StMADS11</i> (three members), <i>MIKC*</i> (three members) and <i>Bs</i> (two members) contain more members than in <i>P. equestris</i> (two members in <i>ANR1</i> and one member in other three clades, respectively) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 23</a>). The <i>ANR1</i> MADS-box gene in Arabidopsis is a key gene involved in regulating lateral development in response to external nitrate supply<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Zhang, H. &amp; Forde, B. G. An Arabidopsis MADS box gene that controls nutrient-induced changes in root architecture. Science 279, 407–409 (1998)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2220">47</a></sup>. Genes in the <i>StMADS11</i> clade have functions in controlling flowering time and inflorescence architecture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Torti, S. &amp; Fornara, F. AGL24 acts in concert with SOC1 and FUL during Arabidopsis floral transition. Plant Signal Behav 7, 1251–1254 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2227">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Liu, C. et al. A conserved genetic pathway determines inflorescence architecture in Arabidopsis and rice. Dev Cell 24, 612–622 (2013)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2230">49</a></sup>. Genes in the <i>Bs</i> clade can regulate seed development and fruit size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Prasad, K. &amp; Ambrose, B. A. Shaping up the fruit: control of fruit size by an Arabidopsis B-sister MADS-box gene. Plant Signal Behav 5, 899–902 (2010)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2238">50</a></sup>. Recent evidence indicated that the closely related MIKC* MADS-domain proteins are important for the functioning of the <i>A. thaliana</i> male gametophyte<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kwantes, M., Liebsch, D. &amp; Verelst, W. How MIKC* MADS-Box Genes Originated and Evidence for Their Conserved Function Throughout the Evolution of Vascular Plant Gametophytes. Mol Biol Evol 29, 293–302 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2245">51</a></sup>. However, genes corresponding to the <i>FLC</i>, <i>AGL12</i> and <i>AGL15</i> clades could not be found in the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome nor in the <i>P. equestris</i> genome. <i>FLC</i> genes have recently been found in cereals, although they have proved difficult to identify because they diverged extensively within a relatively short period<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Ruelens, P. et al. FLOWERING LOCUS C in monocots and the tandem origin of angiosperm-specific MADS-box genes. Nat Commun. 4, 2280 (2013)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2268">52</a></sup>. However, <i>AGL12</i> clade genes are present in the genomes of rice and <i>A. thaliana,</i> while <i>AGL15</i> clade genes are only present in <i>A. thaliana</i>. Therefore, we hypothesise that orthologues of <i>FLC</i>, <i>AGL12</i> and <i>AGL15</i> might have been lost in orchids.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 The number of MADS-box genes in some representative plant species.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/srep19029/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Only 28 putative functional MADS-box type I genes and one pseudogene were found in <i>D. catenatum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 17</a>), suggesting that the <i>D. catenatum</i> type I MADS-box genes have experienced a lower birth rate compared with those of type II MADS-box genes. Tandem gene duplication events seem to have contributed to the increase in type I Mα MADS-box genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 23</a>), indicating that these genes have mainly been duplicated by recent, small-scale duplications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Parenicova, L. et al. Molecular and phylogenetic analyses of the complete MADS-box transcription factor family in Arabidopsis: new openings to the MADS world. Plant Cell 15, 1538–1551 (2003)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2327">53</a></sup>. We found that type I Mα MADS-box genes <i>DcMADS30</i> and <i>DcMADS31</i> and <i>DcMADS57</i> and <i>DcMADS58</i> are located side by side in scaffold12110 and scaffold5677, respectively. In addition, three type I Mα MADS-box genes <i>DcMADS47</i>, <i>DcMADS48</i> and <i>DcMADS50</i> were also found in the same scaffold7526. Interestingly, the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome does not contain any type I Mβ MADS-box genes, although these genes do exist in Arabidopsis, poplar and rice. Interactions among type I MADS-box genes are important for the initiation of endosperm development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Masiero, S., Colombo, L., Grini, P. E., Schnittger, A. &amp; Kater, M. M. The emerging importance of type I MADS box transcription factors for plant reproduction. Plant Cell 23, 865–872 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2357">54</a></sup>. The failed development of endosperm in orchids might be related to the smaller number of type I MADS box genes in the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome.</p><p>In conclusion, <i>Dendrobium</i> represents a fascinating groups of orchids because of their fleshy stem, various flower architectures and synthesis of many kinds of different polysaccharides and the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome sequence forms an important resource for further exploring orchid gene and genome evolution.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Method"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Method</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Sample preparation and sequencing</h3><p>For genome sequencing, we collected leaves, stems and flowers from an individual of wild <i>D. catenatum</i> (voucher specimen : CHINA.Yunnan: Guangnan county, on rock in evergreen broad-leaf forest, alt. 1350 m,10 March, 2010, Z.J. Liu 4979, NOCC) and extracted genomic DNA using a modified CTAB protocol. Sequencing libraries with insert sizes ranging from 180 bp to 20 Kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 1</a>) were constructed using a library construction kit (Illumina, San Diego, CA). These libraries were then sequenced using Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform. The raw reads generated were filtered according to the sequencing quality, presence of adaptor contamination and duplication. Thus, only high-quality reads were used for genome assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome size estimation</h3><p>To estimate the genome size of <i>D. catenatum,</i> we used reads from pair-end libraries to determine the distribution of <i>K</i>-mer values. According to the Lander–Waterman theory<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Lander, E. S. &amp; Waterman, M. S. Genomic mapping by fingerprinting random clones: a mathematical analysis. Genomics 2, 231–239 (1988)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2406">55</a></sup>, the genome size can be determined by the total number of <i>K</i>-mers that were divided by the peak value of the <i>K</i>-mer distribution. Given the high heterozygosity in the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome, we found two peaks in the distribution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). Using the second peak as the expected <i>K</i>-mer depth and the formula Genome size = Total <i>K</i>-mer/Expected <i>K</i>-mer depth, the size of the haploid genome was estimated to be 1.11 Gb (haploid).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Sequence assembly</h3><p>Initially, we used SOAPdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. GigaScience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2440">8</a></sup> to assemble the genome, which produced an assembly of 1.27 Gb with an N50 scaffold size of 80.56 Kb and a corresponding N50 contig size of 6.64 Kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 18</a>). These figures suggest high fragmentation and redundancy. Therefore, to generate a better assembly for further analyses, Platanus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res 24, 1384–1395 (2014)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2447">9</a></sup>, which can effectively manage high-throughput data from heterozygous samples, was used for whole genome shotgun assembly. We subsequently used GapCloser (<a href="http://soap.genomics.cn">http://soap.genomics.cn</a>) to fill gaps remaining after the Platanus built-in gap-filling module had been applied. The final assembly was 1.01 Gb in length, approximately 91% of the estimated genome size, with an N50 scaffold size of 391 Kb and a corresponding N50 contig size of 33.1 Kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Gene and non-coding RNA gene prediction</h3><p>MAKER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Holt, C. &amp; Yandell, M.
