MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 491 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2469">56</a></sup> was used to generate a consensus gene set based on <i>de novo</i> prediction, homology annotation with CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2476">10</a></sup> and other sequenced monocots and RNA-seq gene prediction. These results were integrated into a final set of 28,910 protein-coding genes for annotation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 19</a>). We then generated functional assignments of the <i>D. catenatum</i> genes by aligning their CDS (protein-coding sequences) to sequences available in the public protein databases including KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ogata, H. et al. KEGG: Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes. Nucleic Acids Res 27, 29–34 (1999)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2487">15</a></sup>, SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res 31, 365–370 (2003)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2491">57</a></sup>, TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res 31, 365–370 (2003)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2495">57</a></sup> and InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Zdobnov, E. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan–an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2499">58</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 20</a>). tRNA genes were searched for by tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2506">59</a></sup>. For rRNA identification, we downloaded the Arabidopsis rRNA sequences from NCBI and aligned them against the <i>D. catenatum</i> genome to identify possible rRNAs. Additionally, other types of non-coding RNAs, including miRNA and snRNA, were identified by utilizing INFERNAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335–1337 (2009)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2514">60</a></sup> to search from the Rfam database.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Single nucleotide polymorphisms</h3><p>We used the BWA program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. The Gene Ontology Consortium. Nat Genet 25, 25–29 (2000)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2527">14</a></sup> to remap the pair-end (500 bp) clean reads to the assembled scaffolds. After merging the BAM results, sorting the alignments by the leftmost coordinates and removing potential PCR duplicates, we used SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ogata, H. et al. KEGG: Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes. Nucleic Acids Res 27, 29–34 (1999)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2531">15</a></sup> ‘mpileup’ to identify single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) and short INDELs. We rejected SNPs and InDels within reads with depths lower (&lt;5 folds) or higher (&gt;80 folds) than expected. Filtering was achieved using the vcfutils.pl varFilter tool in the SAMtools package, with parameters -<i>Q</i> 10 -<i>d</i> 5 -<i>D</i> 86. We estimated heterozygosity rates as the density of heterozygous SNPs from the whole genome, gene intervals, introns and exons.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Gene family identification</h3><p>We downloaded genome and annotation data from <i>Amborella</i> (<i>Am.</i>) <i>trichopoda</i> (<a href="http://amborella.huck.psu.edu">http://amborella.huck.psu.edu</a>, version 1.0), <i>Arabidopsis</i> (<i>A.</i>) <i>thaliana</i> (TAIR 10), <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> (purple false brome; Phytozome v9.0), <i>Musaceae acuminata</i> (<a href="http://ensemblgenomes.org">http://ensemblgenomes.org</a>, release-21), <i>Oryza sativa</i> (Nipponbare, IRGSP-1.0), <i>Phoenix</i> (<i>Ph.</i>) <i>dactylifera</i> (<a href="http://qatar-weill.cornell.edu/research/datepalmGenome">http://qatar-weill.cornell.edu/research/datepalmGenome</a>), <i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i> (<a href="ftp://ftp.genomics.org.cn/from_BGISZ/20130120/">ftp://ftp.genomics.org.cn/from_BGISZ/20130120/</a>), <i>Populus</i> (<i>Po.</i>) <i>trichocarpa</i> (<a href="http://ensemblgenomes.org">http://ensemblgenomes.org</a>, release-21), <i>Sorghum</i> (<i>S.</i>) <i>bicolor</i> (sorghum; Phytozome v9.0), <i>Spirodela</i> (<i>Sp.</i>) <i>polyrhiza</i> (common duckweed; <a href="http://www.spirodelagenome.org">http://www.spirodelagenome.org</a>) and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (Phytozome v9.0), <i>Zea mays</i> (<a href="http://www.plantgdb.org/ZmGDB">http://www.plantgdb.org/ZmGDB</a>), <i>Phyllostachys</i> (<i>Phy.</i>) <i>heterocycla</i> (<a href="http://www.bamboogdb.org">http://www.bamboogdb.org</a>). We chose the longest transcript to represent each gene and removed gene models with an open reading frame (ORF) shorter than 150 bp. These protein sets were aligned and clustered using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J., Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of or tholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2695">61</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Phylogenomic dating</h3><p>We conducted phylogenomic dating with PAML McMcTree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Yang, Z. PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Comput Appl Biosci 13, 555–556 (1997)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2707">62</a></sup>. The McMc process was run for 1,500,000 iterations, with a sample frequency of 150 after a burn-in of 500,000 iterations. Other parameters used the default settings of McMcTree. Two independent runs were performed to check convergence. The following constraints were used for time calibrations:</p><ol class="u-list-style-none">
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">1</span>
                      
                        <p>140–150 million years ago (MYA) for the monocot – dicot split<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Gaut, B. S., Morton, B. R., McCaig, B. C. &amp; Clegg, M. T. Substitution rate comparisons between grasses and palms: synonymous rate differences at the nuclear gene Adh parallel rate differences at the plastid gene rbcL. Proc Natl Acad Sci USA 93, 10274–10279 (1996)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2721">63</a></sup>,</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">2</span>
                      
                        <p>94 MYA as the lower boundary for the <i>Vitis</i> – Eurosid split<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Ruelens, P. et al. FLOWERING LOCUS C in monocots and the tandem origin of angiosperm-specific MADS-box genes. Nat Commun. 4, 2280 (2013)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2739">52</a></sup>,</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">3</span>
                      
                        <p>130 MYA as the lower boundary for the Alismatales – Acorales and core monocots (Commelinids, Asparagales, Liliales, etc.) split<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Hsiao, Y. Y. et al. Transcriptomic analysis of floral organs from Phalaenopsis orchid by using oligonucleotide microarray. Gene 518, 91–100 (2013)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2754">64</a></sup>, and</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">4</span>
                      
                        <p>200 MYA as the upper boundary for basal angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, H. et al. Rice MADS6 interacts with the floral homeotic genes SUPERWOMAN1, MADS3, MADS58, MADS13 and DROOPING LEAF in specifying floral organ identities and meristem fate. Plant Cell 23, 2536–2552 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2769">65</a></sup>.</p>
                      
                    </li>
                  </ol><p>Based on these divergence time ranges and the inferred phylogenetic tree, the divergence times between the 12 species were estimated using McMcTree software.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Identification of resistance genes</h3><p>HMMER V3.0 was used to align the protein sequences of <i>D. catenatum</i> against the hidden Markov model of the Pfam NBS (NB-ARC). The TIR and LRR domains were detected by using the Pfam_Scan (−E 0.01 –domE 0.01). MARCOIL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Delorenzi, M. &amp; Speed, T. An HMM model for coiled-coil domains and a comparison with PSSM-based predictions. Bioinformatics 18(4), 617–625 (2002)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2789">66</a></sup> and paircoil2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="McDonnell, A. V., Jiang, T., Keating, A. E. &amp; Berger, B. Paircoil2: improved prediction of coiled coils from sequence. Bioinformatics 22(3), 356–358 (2006)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2793">67</a></sup> were utilized for identification of the CC motif.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Identification of polysaccharide-related genes</h3><p>We collected polysaccharide-related genes of Arabidopsis first by using the CAZY database and other information resources. Then, we performed TBLASTN search against all coding sequences (CDS) datasets of each plant species. These CDS datasets were downloaded from Phytozome (poplar, Selaginella and Physcomitrella), ConGenIE (Norway spruce), QATAR-WEILL.CORNELL (dates palm), RAP-DB (rice) and TAIR (Arabidopsis). In case of <i>Amo. Konjac</i>, RNA-seq data in NCBI SRA (accession number SRX098311) was downloaded and assembled by CLC genomic workbench software. Homologous genes from these species with BLAST E-values less than 1e-5 were then used as BLASTX queries against all protein sequences in Arabidopsis. If the top-hit genes of this BLASTX results belonged to polysaccharide-related genes defined previously, the queries were defined as orthologues in each species. The phylogenetic trees of collected orthologs were constructed by ClustalW<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Thompson, J. D., Gibson, T. J. &amp; Higgins, D. G. Multiple sequence alignment using ClustalW and ClustalX. Curr Protoc Bioinformatics 10.1002/0471250953.bi0203s00 (2002)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2808">68</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Evolution of MADS box genes in <i>D. catenatum</i></h3><p>The MADS-box domain is comprised of 60 amino acids, which we identified for all the potential MADS-box sequences of <i>D. catenatum</i>. Next, we aligned all the MADS-box genes with ClustalW. An un-rooted neighbour-joining phylogenetic tree was constructed in MEGA5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance and maximum parsimony methods. Mol Biol Evol 28, 2731–2739 (2011)." href="/articles/srep19029#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d54135e2826">69</a></sup> with default parameters. Confidence on the tree branches was evaluated by bootstrap analysis (1000 replicates).</p><p>Associated references and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep19029#MOESM1">supplementary information</a> are available in the online version of the paper.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec22" data-title="Additional Information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec22-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec22">Additional Information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec22-content"><p><b>Accession codes</b>: Genome sequences have been submitted to the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI). Whole genome assemblies have been deposited in DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under the accession codes JSDN00000000 (URL: <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/262478">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/262478</a>).</p><p><b>How to cite this article</b>: Zhang, G.-Q. <i>et al.</i> The <i>Dendrobium catenatum</i> Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. <i>Sci. Rep.</i> <b>6</b>, 19029; doi: 10.1038/srep19029 (2016).</p></div></div></section>
